transport system, which operated from 1984 to 1995, was the first commercial maglev system in the world</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Birmingham-Airport-Tower-Emirates-and-Flybe.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Birmingham-Airport-Tower-Emirates-and-Flybe.jpg/220px-Birmingham-Airport-Tower-Emirates-and-Flybe.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Birmingham-Airport-Tower-Emirates-and-Flybe.jpg/330px-Birmingham-Airport-Tower-Emirates-and-Flybe.jpg 1.5x, data-file-height="1224" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Birmingham-Airport-Tower-Emirates-and-Flybe.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The control tower and runway, with aircraft standing at the main terminal building in the foreground.</div></div></div>\n<p><b>Birmingham Airport</b> (<a href="/wiki/IATA_airport_code" title="IATA airport code">IATA</a>: <b>BHX</b>, <a href="/wiki/ICAO_airport_code" title="ICAO airport code">ICAO</a>: <b>EGBB</b>), formerly <i>Birmingham International Airport</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> is an <a href="/wiki/International_airport" title="International airport">international airport</a> located 7 <a href="/wiki/Nautical_mile" title="Nautical mile">nautical miles</a> (13&#160;km; 8.1&#160;mi) east-southeast of <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a> city centre, 9.5 <a href="/wiki/Nautical_mile" title="Nautical mile">nautical miles</a> (17.6&#160;km; 10.9&#160;mi) west-northwest of Coventry slightly north of <a href="/wiki/Bickenhill" title="Bickenhill">Bickenhill</a> village in the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Borough_of_Solihull" title="Metropolitan Borough of Solihull">Metropolitan Borough of Solihull</a>, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>.\n</p><p>Officially opened as <i>Elmdon Airport</i> on 8 July 1939, the airport was requisitioned by the <a href="/wiki/Air_Ministry" title="Air Ministry">Air Ministry</a> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a> and used by both the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> (RAF) and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> as <i>RAF Elmdon</i>. It was largely used for flight training and wartime production purposes. On 8 July 1946, the aerodrome was reopened to civilian operations. \n</p><p>Birmingham Airport currently holds a <a href="/wiki/Civil_Aviation_Authority_(United_Kingdom)" title="Civil Aviation Authority (United Kingdom)">CAA</a> Public Use Aerodrome Licence (Number P451) that allows flights for the public transport of passengers or for flying instruction. Passenger throughput in 2017 was over 12.9 million, making Birmingham the <a href="/wiki/List_of_busiest_airports_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="List of busiest airports in the United Kingdom">seventh busiest airport in the UK</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-stats_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stats-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The airport offers international flights to destinations in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>. Birmingham Airport is an operating base for <a href="/wiki/Jet2.com" title="Jet2.com">Jet2.com</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ryanair" title="Ryanair">Ryanair</a> and <a href="/wiki/TUI_Airways" title="TUI Airways">TUI Airways</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctext">Bicycle</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Location">Location</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmingham_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Location">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Birmingham Airport is 7&#160;<a href="/wiki/Nautical_mile" title="Nautical mile">NM</a> (13&#160;km; 8.1&#160;mi) east-south-east of <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a> city centre, in the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Borough_of_Solihull" title="Metropolitan Borough of Solihull">Metropolitan Borough of Solihull</a>. It is bordered by the <a href="/wiki/National_Exhibition_Centre" title="National Exhibition Centre">National Exhibition Centre</a> to the east, <a href="/wiki/Marston_Green" title="Marston Green">Marston Green</a> to the north, <a href="/wiki/Sheldon,_West_Midlands" title="Sheldon, West Midlands">Sheldon</a> to the west, the village of <a href="/wiki/Bickenhill" title="Bickenhill">Bickenhill</a> to the south, and the village of <a href="/wiki/Elmdon,_West_Midlands" title="Elmdon, West Midlands">Elmdon</a> to the south west.\n</p><p>It is primarily served by the <a href="/wiki/A45_road" title="A45 road">A45</a> main road, and is near Junction 6 of the <a href="/wiki/M42_motorway" title="M42 motorway">M42 motorway</a>. It is connected by the elevated <a href="/wiki/AirRail_Link" class="mw-redirect" title="AirRail Link">AirRail Link</a> with <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_International_railway_station" title="Birmingham International railway station">Birmingham International railway station</a> on the <a href="/wiki/West_Coast_Main_Line" title="West Coast Main Line">West Coast Main Line</a>.\n</p><p>The airport\'s location southeast of the city, plus the only operational runway being northwest\xe2\x80\x93southeast (15/33), means that depending on wind direction, aircraft land or take-off directly over <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a>. The former northeast\xe2\x80\x93southwest runway (06/24) has been incorporated into a <a href="/wiki/Taxiway" title="Taxiway">taxiway</a> for aircraft accessing runway 15/33.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmingham_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Construction_and_opening">Construction and opening</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmingham_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Construction and opening">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1928, the <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_City_Council" title="Birmingham City Council">Birmingham City Council</a> decided that the city required a municipal airport; thus soon thereafter a committee was established to work towards establishing such a facility.<sup id="cite_ref-historiceng_terminal_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historiceng_terminal-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> By 1931, several locations, including <a href="/wiki/Shirley,_West_Midlands" title="Shirley, West Midlands">Shirley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elmdon,_West_Midlands" title="Elmdon, West Midlands">Elmdon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aldridge" title="Aldridge">Aldridge</a>, were reportedly under consideration as potential sites. While Elmdon was considered to be an impressive and appropriate site for the airport, further progress was delayed due to spending cutbacks that had been initiated as a consequence of <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Great Depression in the United Kingdom">the Great Depression</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-historiceng_terminal_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historiceng_terminal-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> By 1933, the project was revived and a new airport committee was formed during the following year to oversee the airport\'s establishment. Prior to any major construction decisions being taken, members of the committee visited various successful airports around Europe in 1935, including <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lyon" title="Lyon">Lyon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a> and <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-historiceng_terminal_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historiceng_terminal-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During January 1935, the airport committee approached British architectural and engineering practice <a href="/wiki/Norman_and_Dawbarn" title="Norman and Dawbarn">Norman and Dawbarn</a>, inviting their attendance and seeking their participation as expert advisers on the airport\'s construction, the practice was subsequently appointed as the project\'s architects.<sup id="cite_ref-historiceng_terminal_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historiceng_terminal-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In 1933, Birmingham City Council authorised the <a href="/wiki/Compulsory_purchase" class="mw-redirect" title="Compulsory purchase">compulsory purchase</a> of 300 acres of land for the use by the airport; another 214 acres were similarly acquired during the following year. During 1936, a <a href="/wiki/Private_bill" class="mw-redirect" title="Private bill">private bill</a> presented by the <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Birmingham Corporation">Birmingham Corporation</a> was passed through <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament</a>, which authorised the acquisition of further land as well as the diversion of various roads and footpaths to permit the airport\'s development. Shortly following the bill\'s passing, various agencies, including the City Engineer and Surveyor, the Public Works Department and a firm of aeronautical consultants, including Norman and Dawbarn, commenced work on preparing the ground, designing both the terminal and hangar buildings, and planning out the airport\'s detailed layout.<sup id="cite_ref-historiceng_terminal_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historiceng_terminal-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By January 1937, Norman and Dawbarn had been authorised to finalise the design drawings; these were apparently completed by June 1937.<sup id="cite_ref-historiceng_terminal_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historiceng_terminal-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In October of that year, various <a href="/wiki/General_contractor" title="General contractor">contractors</a> were appointed to construct various elements of the airport\'s buildings, including its elaborate terminal. Reportedly, the project\'s total expenditure amounted to around \xc2\xa3360,000.<sup id="cite_ref-historiceng_terminal_7-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historiceng_terminal-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Construction work proceeded at a rapid pace; on 1 May 1939, the airport had been completed to such a degree that it was ready to handle traffic.<sup id="cite_ref-historiceng_terminal_7-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historiceng_terminal-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 8 July 1939, the Duchess of Kent, <a href="/wiki/Princess_Marina_of_Greece_and_Denmark" title="Princess Marina of Greece and Denmark">Princess Marina of Greece and Denmark</a>, accompanied by <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">the Prime Minister</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neville_Chamberlain" title="Neville Chamberlain">Neville Chamberlain</a>, officiated at the opening of <i>Elmdon Airport</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pathe_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pathe-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Its terminal, which incorporated the airport\'s air traffic control tower, was designed by Norman and Dawbarn in an <a href="/wiki/Art_Deco" title="Art Deco">Art Deco</a> style; this facility would continue to be used as a terminal until 1984 and subsequently as staff offices and for private flights; it is still intact as of 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-historiceng_terminal_7-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historiceng_terminal-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The airport was owned and operated by <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Birmingham" title="Government of Birmingham">Birmingham City Council</a>. Initial services flew to <a href="/wiki/Croydon_Airport" title="Croydon Airport">Croydon</a>, Glasgow, Liverpool, Ryde, <a href="/wiki/Brighton_City_Airport" title="Brighton City Airport">Shoreham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manchester_Airport" title="Manchester Airport">Manchester</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Southampton" title="Southampton">Southampton</a>; further services were added soon thereafter, although its use as a civilian airport would soon be interrupted by the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-historiceng_terminal_7-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historiceng_terminal-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_World_War">Second World War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmingham_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Second World War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>During the Second World War, Elmdon Airport was requisitioned by the <a href="/wiki/Air_Ministry" title="Air Ministry">Air Ministry</a> and was used by both the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> (RAF) and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> as <i>RAF Elmdon</i>. It was largely operated as an Elementary Flying School and a base for the <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Air_Arm" title="Fleet Air Arm">Fleet Air Arm</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-historiceng_terminal_7-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historiceng_terminal-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> It was during this era that the original grass airstrip was replaced by two hard <a href="/wiki/Runway" title="Runway">runways</a>: 06/24 at 2,469 feet (753&#160;m) and 15/33 at 4,170 feet (1,271&#160;m).<sup id="cite_ref-History_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Large numbers of <a href="/wiki/Avro_Lancaster" title="Avro Lancaster">Avro Lancaster</a> and <a href="/wiki/Short_Stirling" title="Short Stirling">Stirling</a> bombers were manufactured at the <a href="/wiki/Longbridge_plant" title="Longbridge plant">Austin Aero Company</a>\'s shadow factory at <a href="/wiki/Cofton_Hackett" title="Cofton Hackett">Cofton Hackett</a>, but were unable to take off from the short runways at <a href="/wiki/Longbridge" title="Longbridge">Longbridge</a>; thus, they were transported by road to RAF Elmdon, their wings being removed beforehand and re-attached after arrival. They were test flown from the aerodrome and, once declared airworthy, they were flown to their operational units. On 8 July 1946, the aerodrome was reopened to civilian operations, though it remained under government control.<sup id="cite_ref-historiceng_terminal_7-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historiceng_terminal-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-History_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1950_to_1980">1950 to 1980</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmingham_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1950 to 1980">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>During the post-war years, a number of public events, such as air fairs and air races, were held on the site. In 1949, scheduled services began with <a href="/wiki/British_European_Airways" title="British European Airways">British European Airways</a> (BEA) launching routes to Paris; the number of flights to the continent steadily grew over the years, including services to <a href="/wiki/Zurich" class="mw-redirect" title="Zurich">Zurich</a>, <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCsseldorf" title="D\xc3\xbcsseldorf">D\xc3\xbcsseldorf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palma_de_Mallorca" title="Palma de Mallorca">Palma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barcelona" title="Barcelona">Barcelona</a> commencing between 1955 and 1960. During 1960, the City of Birmingham resumed responsibility for the airport\'s operation again, ending central government control.<sup id="cite_ref-History_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1961, an additional terminal building to handle the growing international traffic was opened, which was fittingly called <i>The International Building</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-History_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, work to extend the airport\'s main runway to 7,400&#160;feet (1.4 miles) was undertaken between 1967 and 1970, which permitted the launch of new services using <a href="/wiki/Turboprop" title="Turboprop">turboprop</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jet_propulsion" title="Jet propulsion">jet-powered</a> airliners. Accordingly, a new service to <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York</a> using <a href="/wiki/Vickers_VC10" title="Vickers VC10">VC-10</a> airliner was launched during 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-History_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> By the early 1970s, Birmingham Airport was reportedly handling around one million passengers per year, albeit through a relatively congested passenger terminal. In 1974, the newly-formed <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_Metropolitan_County_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="West Midlands Metropolitan County Council">West Midlands Metropolitan County Council</a> took over management of the airport.<sup id="cite_ref-History_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 16 September 1980, the <a href="/wiki/Supersonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Supersonic">supersonic</a> airliner <a href="/wiki/Concorde" title="Concorde">Concorde</a> made its first visit to Birmingham Airport.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> On 20 October 2003, Concorde made its final visit to the airport as part of <a href="/wiki/Concorde#Retirement" title="Concorde">its farewell tour</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1981_to_2000">1981 to 2000</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmingham_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1981 to 2000">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Birmingham Airport was once home to the world\'s first commercial <a href="/wiki/Maglev" title="Maglev">maglev</a> system in the form of a <a href="/wiki/AirRail_Link#Maglev" class="mw-redirect" title="AirRail Link">low-speed maglev shuttle</a> that ran along a 620-metre line between the terminal and the nearby <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_International_railway_station" title="Birmingham International railway station">Birmingham International railway station</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Following a year of testing and trial use, the Birmingham Airport Maglev was opened to great fanfare during April 1984.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> However, during 1995, the Maglev rail link was discontinued after 11 years; the closure has been attributed to the system\'s unreliability, it having suffered from frequent breakdowns. The original guideway lay dormant but intact for a time, while proposals for its restoration or adaption for other uses were considered.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> In 2003, a replacement <a href="/wiki/Funicular" title="Funicular">cable-hauled</a> system, the <a href="/wiki/AirRail_Link" class="mw-redirect" title="AirRail Link">AirRail Link</a> Cable Liner people mover, was opened, which reused the track and much of the existing infrastructure.<sup id="cite_ref-dcc-bhx_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dcc-bhx-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During 1993, the government limited public sector borrowing came into force and was applied to Birmingham Airport. This change meant that the airport could only expand by using private sector finance. 51% of the local council shares were sold to restructure the airport into a private sector company; this initiative led to the commencement of a \xc2\xa3260 million restructuring programme in 1997.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2000s">2000s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmingham_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 2000s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>During November 2007, Birmingham Airport published a master plan for its development up to 2030, called "Towards 2030: Planning a Sustainable Future for Air Transport in the Midlands".<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> This set out details of changes to the terminals, airfield layout and off-site infrastructure. As with all large scale plans, the proposals were controversial, with opposition from environmentalists and local residents. In particular, the requirement for a second parallel runway based on projected demand was disputed by opponents. Plans for a second runway (a third when demand requires) on the other side of the M42 and a new terminal complex and business park have been published, and they could help to create around 250,000 jobs. It has been estimated that if these plans went ahead, the airport could handle around 70,000,000 passengers annually, and around 500,000 aircraft movements.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In January 2008, the shorter runway (06/24) was decommissioned. It had been used less often due to its short length, noise impact, and its inconvenient position crossing the main runway, making it uneconomic to continue operation. The closure also allowed for apron expansion on both sides of the main runway. However, runway 06/24 remains open as a taxiway and a helicopter airstrip.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In the same month, plans for the extension of the airport runway and the construction of a new air traffic control tower were submitted to <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Borough_of_Solihull" title="Metropolitan Borough of Solihull">Solihull Metropolitan Borough Council</a>.\n</p><p>In June 2008, work began on building the new three-storey International Pier; it was officially opened on 9 September 2009. As part of the airport\'s 70th anniversary, the airport welcomed the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A380" title="Airbus A380">Airbus A380</a> as the first user of the pier. The special service was the first commercial <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A380" title="Airbus A380">A380</a> flight in the UK outside <a href="/wiki/Heathrow_Airport" title="Heathrow Airport">London Heathrow Airport</a>. The new pier is 240&#160;metres long and 24&#160;metres wide. Departing passengers are accommodated on the top level, with arriving passengers on the middle level and office accommodation for airline and handling agents on the ground floor. The new facility provides <a href="/wiki/Jet_bridge" title="Jet bridge">air-bridged</a> aircraft parking for seven wide-bodied aircraft and enough space for 13 smaller aircraft. It can accommodate \'next generation\' environmentally-efficient wide-bodied aircraft such as the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A340-600" class="mw-redirect" title="Airbus A340-600">Airbus A340-600</a>, <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A380" title="Airbus A380">Airbus A380</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747-8" title="Boeing 747-8">Boeing 747-8</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_777X" title="Boeing 777X">Boeing 777X</a>. The new pier also has a new lounge for business class <a href="/wiki/Emirates_(airline)" title="Emirates (airline)">Emirates</a> passengers.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In March 2009, the runway extension plans were approved.<sup id="cite_ref-birminghamairport.co.uk_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birminghamairport.co.uk-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Since_2010">Since 2010</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmingham_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Since 2010">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Birmingham_International_Check_In_area_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Birmingham_International_Check_In_area_1.jpg/220px-Birmingham_International_Check_In_area_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Birmingham_International_Check_In_area_1.jpg/330px-Birmingham_International_Check_In_area_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Birmingham_International_Check_In_area_1.jpg/440px-Birmingham_International_Check_In_area_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Birmingham_International_Check_In_area_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Main check-in hall in Terminal 1</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Departure_lounge,_5am_2_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1176648.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Departure_lounge%2C_5am_2_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1176648.jpg/220px-Departure_lounge%2C_5am_2_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1176648.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Departure_lounge%2C_5am_2_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1176648.jpg/330px-Departure_lounge%2C_5am_2_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1176648.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Departure_lounge%2C_5am_2_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1176648.jpg/440px-Departure_lounge%2C_5am_2_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1176648.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Departure_lounge,_5am_2_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1176648.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Departure lounge area</div></div></div>\n<p>In September 2010, it was announced that after the merging of Terminals 1 and 2 into a single facility in 2011, the airport would drop the "International" from its official name to become "Birmingham Airport\'".<sup id="cite_ref-namechange_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-namechange-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> A Midlands-based marketing agency was recruited to "create a new corporate identity that reflects Birmingham Airport\'s current position in the market place, as well as its future potential". Figures from Birmingham Airport show that 8 million people live within a one hour\'s drive of the airport, but less than 40% of them use it. It is hoped that the rebrand will make the airport "more visible to the market".<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> In November 2010, the new name started to be used.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The new logo, interlocking circles in shades of blue, and slogan, "Hello World", were designed to reflect the airport\'s new positioning as a global travel hub.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In January 2011 the viewing gallery, \'Aviation Experience And <a href="/wiki/Gift_shop" title="Gift shop">Gift Shop</a>\', closed indefinitely.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In the same month, the airport merged its two terminals into a single terminal building, which involved the construction of two additional floors. A new lower ground floor accommodates the new Arrivals and Meet &amp; Greet area, while the 3rd floor was built in the Millennium Link and the two terminals to accommodate the new Centralised Security Search area. In July 2011, construction of a new control tower began.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The new control tower was completed in March 2012; it replaced the airport\'s original tower, which had been used since the airport opened in 1939.\n</p><p>On 23 February 2011, Birmingham Airport announced that the <a href="/wiki/High_Speed_2" title="High Speed 2">High Speed 2</a> railway could be a solution to runway capacity problems in London; management figures suggested that it would be quicker to get to London from Birmingham than from <a href="/wiki/London_Stansted_Airport" title="London Stansted Airport">Stansted Airport</a> once completed, and claimed that the airport had capacity for nine million more passengers.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Plans for the extension of the airport\'s runway, and the construction of the new air traffic control tower, were submitted to <a href="/wiki/Solihull_Metropolitan_Borough_Council" title="Solihull Metropolitan Borough Council">Solihull Metropolitan Borough Council</a> in January 2008, and approved in March 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-birminghamairport.co.uk_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birminghamairport.co.uk-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Originally, the target for completion was in time for the <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Olympics" title="2012 Summer Olympics">2012 London Olympics and Paralympics</a>. An Olympic ceremony was held at the airport on 23 April 2012. The Olympic rings were unveiled on the tower and could be seen from the <a href="/wiki/A45_road" title="A45 road">A45 road</a> and the main terminal building. This was to commemorate the build-up to the <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Olympics" title="2012 Summer Olympics">London 2012</a> Olympic Games. These rings were removed once the Olympic Games officially closed, just before the 2012 Summer Paralympics began. Work on the new runway eventually began in autumn 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-businesstraveller_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-businesstraveller-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The extension to the southern end of the runway originally required the <a href="/wiki/A45_road" title="A45 road">A45 Coventry Road</a> to be diverted into a tunnel under the extended section, but to cut costs, it was diverted south of the runway instead.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In Summer 2013 the new air traffic control tower became fully operational;<sup id="cite_ref-:0_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> the old carriageway of the A45 was closed and the new carriageway was opened.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC2012-11-28_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2012-11-28-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> In May 2014, the 400-metre runway extension was officially opened;<sup id="cite_ref-businesstraveller_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-businesstraveller-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> the full length was first used on 22 July 2014, when <a href="/wiki/China_Southern_Airlines" title="China Southern Airlines">China Southern Airlines</a> operated its first <a href="/wiki/Air_charter" title="Air charter">charter</a> between Birmingham and <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-businesstraveller_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-businesstraveller-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ontario_Teachers%27_Pension_Plan" title="Ontario Teachers&#39; Pension Plan">Ontario Teachers\' Pension Plan</a>, a Canadian institutional investor, increased its stake in the airport to 48.25% in early 2015. It also owns 100% of <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Airport" title="Bristol Airport">Bristol Airport</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Birmingham handled over 11.6 million passengers in 2016, a record total for the airport, making it the <a href="/wiki/List_of_busiest_airports_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="List of busiest airports in the United Kingdom">seventh busiest UK airport</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-stats_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stats-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 28 September 2016, \xc2\xa3100 million of investment was allocated to a new baggage handling system and two new car parks, including a drop-off car park.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>British regional airline <a href="/wiki/Flybe" title="Flybe">Flybe</a> operated an extensive route network from Birmingham to destinations within the United Kingdom and mainland Europe until it entered administration on 4 March 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-stats_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stats-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="COVID-19 pandemic in the United Kingdom">2020 COVID-19 pandemic</a>, a temporary mortuary was established in a hangar at the airport, with space for 12,000 bodies.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC-52202766_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-52202766-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Facilities_and_infrastructure">Facilities and infrastructure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmingham_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Facilities and infrastructure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Birmingham Airport\'s current terminal complex combines the two old terminals via the Millennium Link in to one large terminal, with gates 1-20 in the old Terminal 2, and all other gates in the old Terminal 1. \n</p><p>Terminal 1 was opened on 3 April 1984, seventeen years after the original plans to construct a new terminal to ease congestion in the original Elmdon Terminal (<a href="/wiki/Listed_building#Categories_of_listed_buildings" title="Listed building">Grade II listed</a> since August 2018 and used for private and official flights).<sup id="cite_ref-telegraph-20180822_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph-20180822-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NHLE-elmdon_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHLE-elmdon-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Since then, T1 has been extended multiple times to accommodate the increase in both passenger numbers and aircraft movements.\n</p><p>Terminal 2 (also known as Eurohub) was opened in 1991 and was originally served by <a href="/wiki/British_Airways" title="British Airways">British Airways</a> and several European carriers including <a href="/wiki/Air_France" title="Air France">Air France</a> and <a href="/wiki/KLM" title="KLM">KLM</a> for a mixture of domestic and European flights. \n</p><p>All check in desks and arrivals are on the ground floor. The central security area, along with airside shops and restaurants are located on the first floor.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Airlines_and_destinations">Airlines and destinations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmingham_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: data-file-width="300" data-file-height="300" /> 2.4%\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Accidents_and_incidents">Accidents and incidents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmingham_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Accidents and incidents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><span class="vevent">19&#160;January&#160;1973<span style="display:none">&#160;(<span class="bday dtstart published updated">1973-01-19</span>)</span>: <span class="summary">A <a href="/wiki/Vickers_Viscount" title="Vickers Viscount">Vickers Viscount</a> passenger jet G-AZLR inbound from Leeds Bradford Airport suffered a severe port undercarriage failure upon landing.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup></span></span></li>\n<li><span class="vevent">23&#160;February&#160;2006<span style="display:none">&#160;(<span class="bday dtstart published updated">2006-02-23</span>)</span>: <span class="summary"><a href="/wiki/Mahan_Air" title="Mahan Air">Mahan Air</a> <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A310" title="Airbus A310">Airbus A310</a> operating a flight from <a href="/wiki/Tehran_Imam_Khomeini_International_Airport" title="Tehran Imam Khomeini International Airport">Tehran</a>, Iran, was involved in a serious incident while on approach to Birmingham International Airport. The aircraft descended to the published minimum descent altitude of 740&#160;ft despite still being 11&#160;nm from the runway threshold. At a point 6&#160;nm from the runway the aircraft had descended to an altitude of 660&#160;ft, which was 164&#160;ft above ground level. Having noticed the descent profile, Birmingham <a href="/wiki/Air_traffic_control" title="Air traffic control">air traffic control</a> issued an immediate climb instruction to the aircraft, however, the crew had already commenced a <a href="/wiki/Missed_approach" title="Missed approach">missed approach</a>, having received a <a href="/wiki/Ground_proximity_warning_system" title="Ground proximity warning system">GPWS</a> alert. The aircraft was radar vectored for a second approach during which the flight crew again initiated an early descent. On this occasion, the radar controller instructed the crew to maintain their altitude and the crew successfully completed the approach to a safe landing. The accident investigation determined that the primary cause was use of the incorrect <a href="/wiki/Distance_measuring_equipment" class="mw-redirect" title="Distance measuring equipment">DME</a> for the approach, combined with a substantial breakdown in the <a href="/wiki/Crew_resource_management" title="Crew resource management">Crew Resource Management</a>. Three safety recommendations were made.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup></span></span></li>\n<li><span class="vevent">15&#160;June&#160;2006<span style="display:none">&#160;(<span class="bday dtstart published updated">2006-06-15</span>)</span>: <span class="summary">A <a href="/wiki/ASL_Airlines_Belgium" title="ASL Airlines Belgium">TNT Airways</a> cargo <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_Classic#737-300" title="Boeing 737 Classic">737-300</a> made an emergency landing at Birmingham with damaged landing gear.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The aircraft, registration OO-TND, had been flying from <a href="/wiki/Li%C3%A8ge" title="Li\xc3\xa8ge">Li\xc3\xa8ge</a> in Belgium to <a href="/wiki/London_Stansted_Airport" title="London Stansted Airport">London\xe2\x80\x93Stansted</a>. Due to poor visibility at Stansted the flight diverted to <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands_Airport" title="East Midlands Airport">East Midlands Airport</a>. As the weather at East Midlands was also poor, the aircraft performed a full autopilot approach. During this approach the autopilot momentarily disengaged causing it to deviate from the course. The aircraft hit the grass to the side of the runway, which caused the right main gear to detach. The crew initiated a go-around, declared an emergency and diverted to Birmingham. After it landed on Birmingham\'s main runway, the airport was closed for a number of hours. The pilots were unharmed.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> However, the company ascribed the incident to human error and both pilots were dismissed.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The official report into the accident highlighted a number of factors contributing to the accident \xe2\x80\x93 poor weather forecast information; a message passed from the <a href="/wiki/Air_traffic_control" title="Air traffic control">Air Traffic Control</a> to the aircraft at an "inappropriate" time; the pilot accidentally disconnecting the <a href="/wiki/Autopilot" title="Autopilot">autopilot</a> when attempting to respond to the message; the pilot losing "situational awareness" and failing to abort the landing.<sup id="cite_ref-report_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-report-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup></span></span> Follow this link for a more detailed report and Official reports from the AAIB.<sup id="cite_ref-aviation_safety.net_report_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aviation_safety.net_report-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><span class="vevent">19&#160;November&#160;2010<span style="display:none">&#160;(<span class="bday dtstart published updated">2010-11-19</span>)</span>: <span class="summary">A <a href="/wiki/Cessna_Citation_family" title="Cessna Citation family">Cessna Citation</a> aircraft, registration G-VUEM, crashed at Birmingham Airport during final approach in thick fog. Reports from West Midlands Police were that there were two casualties, one critical. The aircraft was bringing a human liver from <a href="/wiki/Belfast_International_Airport" title="Belfast International Airport">Belfast Airport</a>, for a transplant operation which was subsequently completed successfully.<sup id="cite_ref-Liver_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liver-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The airport reopened at around midday the following day.</span></span> Follow this link for a more detailed report and Official reports from the AAIB.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Security_incidents">Security incidents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmingham_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Security incidents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><span class="vevent">6&#160;June&#160;2007<span style="display:none">&#160;(<span class="bday dtstart published updated">2007-06-06</span>)</span>: <span class="summary">The <i><a href="/wiki/Tonight_(1999_TV_programme)" title="Tonight (1999 TV programme)">Tonight with Trevor McDonald</a></i> programme exposed serious security flaws at Birmingham Airport over six months. Fifteen members of staff working for the security contractor "ICTS UK Ltd" were suspended and subsequently dismissed for gross misconduct.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Members of security were filmed asleep on duty, reading magazines whilst operating x-ray scanners, leaving aircraft unguarded, and ignoring bags sent for extra security checks, as well as being understaffed. The security lapse was deemed so serious, that <a href="/wiki/Bennie_Thompson" title="Bennie Thompson">Bennie Thompson</a>, the chairman of the US Congress <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Homeland_Security" title="United States House Committee on Homeland Security">Homeland Security Committee</a>, commented on it in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> and advised that all flights to and from Birmingham Airport should cease.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> ICTS dismissed the members of staff shown in the programme for their actions, but still claimed that the footage had been "contrived to exaggerate and sensationalise" the issues.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup></span></span></li>\n<li><span class="vevent">8&#160;June&#160;2009<span style="display:none">&#160;(<span class="bday dtstart published updated">2009-06-08</span>)</span>: <span class="summary">The <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_Police" title="West Midlands Police">West Midlands Police</a> helicopter (G-WMAO) was destroyed by arsonists,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> and subsequently written off.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> A year later, a new <a href="/wiki/Eurocopter_EC135" title="Eurocopter EC135">Eurocopter EC135</a> similar to G-WMAO was handed over to West Midlands Police at the <a href="/wiki/Farnborough_Airshow" title="Farnborough Airshow">Farnborough Airshow</a>. Thousands of pounds were subsequently spent upgrading security surrounding the police helicopter.</span></span><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><span class="vevent">17&#160;July&#160;2014<span style="display:none">&#160;(<span class="bday dtstart published updated">2014-07-17</span>)</span>: <span class="summary">A member of the public got onto the airfield through a restricted area of the terminal by crawling through the opening of a <a href="/wiki/Baggage_carousel" title="Baggage carousel">baggage carousel</a> and getting onto the airport\'s tarmac apron, and then got aboard a <a href="/wiki/Lufthansa" title="Lufthansa">Lufthansa</a> <a href="/wiki/Embraer_E-Jet_family" title="Embraer E-Jet family">Embraer 195</a> plane. He was subsequently fined.</span></span><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC-28864442_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-28864442-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ground_transport">Ground transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span joins the railway station to the airport, operated by a track and pulley system</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Birmingham_interchange.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Birmingham_interchange.png/220px-Birmingham_interchange.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Birmingham_interchange.png/330px-Birmingham_interchange.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Birmingham_interchange.png/440px-Birmingham_interchange.png 2x" data-file-width="1116" data-file-height="716" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Birmingham_interchange.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The proposed \'Birmingham Interchange\'</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_transport">Public transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmingham_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Public transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rail">Rail</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmingham_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Rail">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Birmingham Airport is served by <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_International_railway_station" title="Birmingham International railway station">Birmingham International railway station</a>. The station is on the <a href="/wiki/West_Coast_Main_Line" title="West Coast Main Line">West Coast Main Line</a> between <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a> and <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, and trains are operated by <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_Trains" title="West Midlands Trains">West Midlands Trains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avanti_West_Coast" title="Avanti West Coast">Avanti West Coast</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transport_for_Wales_Rail_Services" title="Transport for Wales Rail Services">TfW Rail</a>, and <a href="/wiki/CrossCountry" title="CrossCountry">CrossCountry</a>. Access between the railway station and the airport terminal is provided by free <a href="/wiki/AirRail_Link" class="mw-redirect" title="AirRail Link">AirRail Link</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_High_Speed_2">Proposed High Speed 2</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmingham_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Proposed High Speed 2">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>As part of Phase 1 of the <a href="/wiki/High_Speed_2" title="High Speed 2">High Speed 2</a> rail link, a new railway station called <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_Interchange" title="Birmingham Interchange">Birmingham Interchange</a> will be built to serve both the airport and the <a href="/wiki/National_Exhibition_Centre" title="National Exhibition Centre">National Exhibition Centre</a>. The station will be built on the far side of the <a href="/wiki/M42_motorway" title="M42 motorway">M42 motorway</a> and connect to the airport using a "rapid transit <a href="/wiki/People_mover" title="People mover">people mover</a>". <a href="/wiki/High_Speed_2" title="High Speed 2">High Speed 2</a> is currently planned for completion by 2026.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="West_Midlands_Metro">West Midlands Metro</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmingham_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: West Midlands Metro">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>There are plans to implement a new Line 3 of the <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_Metro" title="West Midlands Metro">West Midlands Metro</a> to Birmingham Airport from <a href="/wiki/Edgbaston" title="Edgbaston">Edgbaston</a>, this would link <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_Interchange" title="Birmingham Interchange">Birmingham Interchange</a>, <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_International_railway_station" title="Birmingham International railway station">Birmingham International</a> and <a class="mw-selflink selflink">Birmingham Airport</a> to the City Centre of <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a> in 45 minutes travelling along the A45. <sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bus_and_coach">Bus and coach</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmingham_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Bus and coach">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/National_Express_West_Midlands" title="National Express West Midlands">National Express West Midlands</a> operates the main bus routes calling at Birmingham Airport, those being the X1 to <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_city_centre" title="Birmingham city centre">Birmingham city centre</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coventry" title="Coventry">Coventry</a>, and the X12 to <a href="/wiki/Chelmsley_Wood" title="Chelmsley Wood">Chelmsley Wood</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solihull" title="Solihull">Solihull</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Other smaller operators also call at the airport. Bus stops are situated outside Terminal One.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Most buses are operated by <a href="/wiki/National_Express_West_Midlands" title="National Express West Midlands">National Express West Midlands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/National_Express_Coaches" title="National Express Coaches">National Express Coaches</a> operates various long distance coaches calling at Birmingham Airport on the way to or from <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_Coach_Station" title="Birmingham Coach Station">Birmingham Coach Station</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxi">Taxi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmingham_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Taxi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Hackney_carriage" title="Hackney carriage">Black cabs</a> are available at the <a href="/wiki/Taxicab_stand" title="Taxicab stand">taxi-rank</a> outside the arrivals area of the terminal.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Car">Car</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmingham_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Car">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Birmingham Airport is accessible from the north and south via Junction Six of the <a href="/wiki/M42_motorway" title="M42 motorway">M42 motorway</a>. From <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a> city centre, the A45 runs directly to the airport. Charges apply in some areas even for very short periods of time, with locations farther from the airport being cheaper than those near the airport.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bicycle">Bicycle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmingham_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Bicycle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The only cycle route available heads south over the A45 travelling towards <a href="/wiki/Solihull" title="Solihull">Solihull</a>. Birmingham Airport has however published "recommended routes" for cyclists.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Free short term cycle parking is available close to the terminal. For longer stays, bicycles must be stored in the <a href="/wiki/Baggage" title="Baggage">Left Luggage</a> for a charge.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmingham_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Birmingham" title="Transport in Birmingham">Transport in Birmingham</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Birmingham_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<style
